  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_9e2a0e81-3b1f-4c07-9fb1-c3bef1ffa29a'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer3644457964898501337">Originally published in 1965, <i>The Painted Bird</i> established Jerzy Kosinski as a major literary figure. Called by the <i>Los Angeles Times</i> "one of the most imposing novels of the decade," it was eventually translated into more than thirty languages.<br/><br/>A harrowing story that follows the wanderings of a boy abandoned by his parents during World War II, <i>The Painted Bird</i> is a dark nov</span>
  <span id="freeText3644457964898501337" style="display:none">Originally published in 1965, <i>The Painted Bird</i> established Jerzy Kosinski as a major literary figure. Called by the <i>Los Angeles Times</i> "one of the most imposing novels of the decade," it was eventually translated into more than thirty languages.<br /><br />A harrowing story that follows the wanderings of a boy abandoned by his parents during World War II, <i>The Painted Bird</i> is a dark novel that examines the proximity of terror and savagery to innocence and love. It is the first, and the most famous, novel by a writer who is now discredited.</span>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_48496324" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/48496324" />
    <a title="Paul Bryant" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/416390-paul-bryant"><img alt="Paul Bryant" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1224113172p2/416390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/48496324?book_show_action=true">Mar 07, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul Bryant" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul Bryant" href="/user/show/416390-paul-bryant">Paul Bryant</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/416390-paul-bryant?shelf=novels">novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/416390-paul-bryant?shelf=holocaust-literature">holocaust-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer48496324" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4265021665234755357">Reading this one is like opening an oven door and the WHITE HOT BLAST OF HATRED from every page sears your flesh, scars your brain, and when you finish it you cram it shut with relief and throw it quickly into a box marked ‚ÄúCharity‚Äù although giving this to anyone would not be any kind of charitable act unless they need something to keep the fire going. What kind of a shitstorm do we have here? <br/> For some reason I thought this was the story of a kid caught up in the Holocaust, i.e. a ghetto and co</span>
  <span id="freeText4265021665234755357" style="display:none">Reading this one is like opening an oven door and the WHITE HOT BLAST OF HATRED from every page sears your flesh, scars your brain, and when you finish it you cram it shut with relief and throw it quickly into a box marked ‚ÄúCharity‚Äù although giving this to anyone would not be any kind of charitable act unless they need something to keep the fire going. What kind of a shitstorm do we have here? <br /> For some reason I thought this was the story of a kid caught up in the Holocaust, i.e. a ghetto and concentration camp story. But it isn‚Äôt. It‚Äôs the story of 7 year old kid (not named) who is sent to the remote Polish countryside by his parents in an attempt <i>to keep him safe</i>. Fat chance of that. The parents appear to have been a little over-optimistic. The kid avoids the Germans, mostly, but he can‚Äôt avoid the Poles. For the next five years he hops from one ghastly peasant village to another, being taken in by a series of grotesque caricatures - psychopaths, sadists, rapists the lot of them. Every Polish peasant immediately takes him for a Gypsy and from then on thinks it‚Äôs okay ‚Äì almost compulsory - to inflict the maximum torment their tiny Polish peasant brains can imagine. Did I say Polish peasant? Yes, very specifically : this book is a hymn of hate to the Polish peasant. There‚Äôs only a handful of Germans in the whole 285 pages and one of those is quite kindly . What this novel is saying in a SCREECHINGLY LOUD voice is that <i>you couldn‚Äôt have found a better place for your Holocaust than Poland</i> ‚Äì everyone truly madly <i>deeply</i> hated the Jews ‚Äì and the Gypsies. They really did. They sat around and gleefully told each other that at last the Jews were getting their comeuppance. <br /><br />Historical note : of the 5.8 to 6 million Jews murdered in the Holocaust, 75% came from either Poland or Russia. 90% of all Polish Jews were murdered. ALL of the six extermination camps (as opposed to concentration camps) were in Poland.<br /><br />Cinematic note : Claude Lanzmann says the same thing as Jerzy in his epic documentary ‚ÄúShoah‚Äù.<br /><br />Back to the book. There‚Äôs a big problem with it. Actually, there are about ten big problems, but the biggest is credibility. Is all this stuff to be believed? I mean, come on, Jerzy! This phantasmagoria of bestiality, rape, murder, torture, more rape, incest, beating, this unceasing onslaught directed against this small boy? Here‚Äôs a few quotes :<br /><br /><i>Without saying a word Garbos used to beat me unexpectedly (p154)<br /><br />I did as I was told but he continued the beatings. (p155)<br /><br />As soon as the priest had left, Garbos took me inside, stripped me and flogged me with a willow switch (p156)<br /><br />Garbos would practice at first casually and then more eagerly new ways of flogging me with a willow cane (p159)<br /><br />‚Ä¶he beat me and kicked me until he was out of breath (p160)<br /><br />I went round in a daze and was beaten for neglecting my work (p160)<br /><br />He thought I was mocking him on purpose and I got an even worse beating (p161)<br /><br />He started to beat me more often (p165)<br /><br />he was beating me harder than usual (p166)</i><br /><br />Okay, this chapter has more beats per minute than most, but there‚Äôs at least one gross outrage inflicted on this kid once every five pages or so, either by adults or by every other kid he encounters. You get the idea that this must have been one aggravating revolting brat of a kid. No one even smiles at him until page 213. You get so you aren‚Äôt taking this stuff as seriously as you really know you should be. You catch yourself wincing and saying <i>‚ÄúEwwww, that was a good one!‚Äù</i> like when Linda Blair's head goes round in The Exorcist.<br /><br />Credibility : I kind of think that fiction should tell the truth, but I also know it‚Äôs made up. Hmmm. Serious fiction should tell the truth about humans, because unserious fiction just peddles the lies, myths and distortions we‚Äôre all too familiar with. So if you‚Äôre writing about Holocaust racism, as Kosinski is, you should make your story credible. I don‚Äôt want to be saying hey, ten pages without a rape-murder, I bet there‚Äôs one coming up soon ‚Äì oh, here it is. In this way The painted Bird resembles something like Justine by de Sade ‚Äì no plot, no characters, just lots of gruesome vignettes strung together. The reader is stultified. <br /> Such a sensational novel brought Kosinski a lot of attention, and when you take a look at this guy, he turns out to be very interesting. He was like one of those 19th century ‚Äúadventurers‚Äù, guys with dubious backgrounds who suddenly explode through the social firmament, charming and wowing the glitterati, then being revealed as frauds and charlatans. By subterfuge JK got himself out of communist Poland and within five minutes he‚Äôd created this best-seller in his second language. I say "created" because there‚Äôs some doubt about whether he actually wrote it. JK let it be understood that TPB might be autobiographical, and most of its first readers and reviewers accepted it as such ‚Äì right now Amazon quotes the Merriam-Webster Encyclopaedia of Literature : ‚ÄúThe ordeals of the central character parallel Kosinski's own experiences during World War II.‚Äù But this was exposed in 1996 as a big fat lie when a biographer discovered that Jewish Jerzy and his family lived in Poland together throughout the war <i>protected by all their Polish friends</i>. No brutality, no ghettos for JK. So the best guess might be that JK took most of the stuff in his novel from unidentified Polish-language accounts of survival during the war, then paid translators to help him render the material into English. This isn‚Äôt a bad thing, but it isn‚Äôt the last word in scrupulousness either. Critics have noted that all of JK‚Äôs novels are in different styles because he always worked with different translators and editors. (or, it has been whispered, different ghost-writers.)<br /> Back in Poland the communists banned it, which is nicely ironic as the adult who rescues the boy, finally, and becomes a father to him, briefly, is a communist and teaches the kid all about the workers‚Äô struggle. But you can see their point ‚Äì this is an anti-Polish novel ‚Äì no, an anti-Polish-peasant novel ‚Äì no, an anti-Polish-peasant-during-WW2 novel ‚Äì well, definitely one of those.<br /> In the end, this novel is a failure. But it's a brave, reckless, dangerous, blazing failure.<br /> Cinematic PS : I was watching Reds the other week and was mightily impressed by the actor playing Zinoviev. He had a hell of a face, hell of a haircut and a delivery that made his few scenes the most memorable of the 3 hour movie. And yes, that actor was Jerzy Kosinski. What a geezer!<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4265021665234755357" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_416965753" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/416965753" />
    <a title="Emily May" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4622890-emily-may"><img alt="Emily May" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528390751p2/4622890.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/416965753?book_show_action=true">Sep 18, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emily May" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emily May" href="/user/show/4622890-emily-may">Emily May</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4622890-emily-may?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4622890-emily-may?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4622890-emily-may?shelf=2012">2012</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer416965753" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7847851966834718231"><b>Warning: I talk about a really gross and disturbing scene from the book in this review, please do not read if you're going to be upset and/or offended by talk of graphic sexual violence.</b><br/><br/>This book is one of my dad's favourite books of all time, I don't know how many years he's been telling me to read it now and we've always had similar opinions on books before. But <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18452.The_Painted_Bird' title='The Painted Bird by Jerzy Kosi≈Ñski' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Painted Bird</a> did not live up to my expectations and the whole idea of it just left a very bad taste in my mouth.<br/><br/>Pretty much anyon</span>
  <span id="freeText7847851966834718231" style="display:none"><b>Warning: I talk about a really gross and disturbing scene from the book in this review, please do not read if you're going to be upset and/or offended by talk of graphic sexual violence.</b><br /><br />This book is one of my dad's favourite books of all time, I don't know how many years he's been telling me to read it now and we've always had similar opinions on books before. But <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18452.The_Painted_Bird" title="The Painted Bird by Jerzy Kosi≈Ñski" rel="nofollow noopener">The Painted Bird</a> did not live up to my expectations and the whole idea of it just left a very bad taste in my mouth.<br /><br />Pretty much anyone who's ever had some level of history education will have heard of some of the atrocities committed during the Holocaust, no matter how many times you read about human beings burning the children of other human beings and watching people slowly starve to death because of their race, religion or other factors, it is still just as shocking and horrifying. One of the areas sometimes neglected in these accounts of wartime cruelty is the suffering of non-German Jews and it may come as a shock to some to learn that more than 70% of those Jews murdered during Hitler's reign were actually from Poland and Russia. Also, all six extermination camps - as opposed to concentration camps - were located in Poland. [ <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/48496324" rel="nofollow noopener">*</a> ]<br /><br />So when Jerzy Kosinski came stumbling out of communist Poland with a story about a young boy who was sent to the Polish countryside by his parents to hopefully protect him from the horrors going on in that area of Europe during this time, a young boy who moved from remote village to remote village, finding and enduring the worst kind of horrors imaginable along the way, most of Kosinski's readers wrongly assumed that this book was autobiographical. Rather than correcting his audience, Mr Kosinski rode the wave of popularity and did nothing to change these misconceptions. I suppose if someone wanted to give me a million for something I hadn't done I'd probably take the money and run too, so I don't really care that the author wasn't more vocal with the truth of this book. But... what I do think is that the knowledge that this novel is complete fiction - even if this stuff did happen somewhere - turns a potentially moving tale into a gratuitous torturefest.<br /><br />Just to compare this with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/278232.S___A_Novel_About_the_Balkans_" title="S. (A Novel About the Balkans) by Slavenka Drakuliƒá" rel="nofollow noopener">S.</a> - another fictional book about atrocities committed during war - I'm not saying that people cannot write successful fictional stories set during the holocaust or that it needs to be a memoir to be effective. But, where <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/278232.S___A_Novel_About_the_Balkans_" title="S. (A Novel About the Balkans) by Slavenka Drakuliƒá" rel="nofollow noopener">S.</a> is a deeply moving tale that focuses on the internal effect had by the abuse which the captors inflicted upon their victims, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18452.The_Painted_Bird" title="The Painted Bird by Jerzy Kosi≈Ñski" rel="nofollow noopener">The Painted Bird</a> tells of a series of gruesome acts that vary from extreme beatings, to brutal rape scenes, to a man gouging out another man's eye with a spoon... and you have to ask yourself what he achieved other than making you feel physically ill at times. Pointless, mindless, disgusting scenes of violence that seem to me to be nothing but shock tactics. <br /><br />After a while of reading all these disturbing scenes, you start to feel like you're in a Saw movie, like the author is trying to create scenarios that are each more repulsive than the last just to play with the characters a bit more, make their lives a bit worse. Like raping a woman with a glass bottle and then kicking her abdomen until the glass shatters and she bleeds to death. And I do not mind reading gross scenes of violence as long as I feel it contributes something and isn't just there to keep me wide-eyed long enough that I forget the book isn't very well written and there's been no character development. I find it somewhat insulting to all those people who genuinely suffered during the holocaust that Kosinski would use it in a such an awful, emotionally-manipulative way.<br /><br />I feel like if I'd really wanted to experience violence, torture and rape without being moved in any way, then I could have just watched Game of Thrones. At least that has hot men for me to look at.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7847851966834718231" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_776116409" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/776116409" />
    <a title="Glenn Russell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23385697-glenn-russell"><img alt="Glenn Russell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1505195595p2/23385697.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/776116409?book_show_action=true">Nov 28, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Glenn Russell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Glenn Russell" href="/user/show/23385697-glenn-russell">Glenn Russell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780553265200" href="/book/show/839806.The_Painted_Bird">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer776116409" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15531342571351736492"><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1504574084i/23786681.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>The cover of the Mass Market Paperback edition from the 1970s of <i>The Painted Bird</i> features a small section of Hieronymus Bosch hell-landscape -- dressed in sickly green and wearing a white hood, a creature with a man's body and head of a long-beaked bird walks on crutches carrying a large wicker basket on its back, and in the basket a small black devil with spiky fingers touches the shoulder of a wary young boy as he whispers into the boy's ear. This is an apt cover for Jerzy Kosinski's fictiona</span>
  <span id="freeText15531342571351736492" style="display:none"><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1504574084i/23786681.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The cover of the Mass Market Paperback edition from the 1970s of <i>The Painted Bird</i> features a small section of Hieronymus Bosch hell-landscape -- dressed in sickly green and wearing a white hood, a creature with a man's body and head of a long-beaked bird walks on crutches carrying a large wicker basket on its back, and in the basket a small black devil with spiky fingers touches the shoulder of a wary young boy as he whispers into the boy's ear. This is an apt cover for Jerzy Kosinski's fictionalized autobiographical novel set in Poland during the reign of Nazi terror in World War 11.<br><br>I first read this harrowing tale thirty-five years ago. I have read many dark, disturbing novels filled with brutality of every stripe, including such works as Malamud's <i>The Fixer</i>, Dostoyevsky's <i>The House of the Dead</i>, and Solzhenitsyn's <i>Gulag Archipelago</i>, but, in my view, perhaps because the narrator is a ten year old boy, no novel has its main character live through a more painful hell than in <i>The Painted Bird</i>.<br><br>Several months after reading this novel, the author himself made a visit to a large bookstore in Philadelphia for a book-signing, so I had an opportunity to actually meet him - a small man with a thin, high pitched voice and sharp, chiseled fine features, a man who struck me as being both sensitive and friendly. He appreciated my words of thanks and told me, when asked, that he was heading to New Orleans and expected to have some exciting times.<br><br>Anyway, that was then. Several days ago I saw my local library had a copy of <i>The Painted Bird</i> audio book and immediately checked it out. I started also rereading the printed book as I listened to the audio. The reader, Fred Berman, did his homework - his accent and inflection and manner of speaking is spot-on Jerzy Kosinski.<br><br>If you are unfamiliar, this story is of an orphan boy with black eyes and sharp nose, labeled gypsy-Jew, forced to wander from village to village, subjected physically to beatings, rape, tortures, as well as murder attempts, while subjected psychologically to being treated as a messenger of the devil and an evil spirit who casts spells with a glance from his black eyes.<br><br>The boy is so traumatized from unrelenting abuse, he completely losses his capacity to speak for many months. The abuse reaches such a pitch, at one point he reflects on the nature of evil: "I tried to visualize the manner in which the evil spirits operated. The minds and souls of people were as open to these forces as a plowed field, and it was on this field that the Evil Ones incessantly scattered their malignant seed. If their seed sprouted to life, if they felt welcomed, they offered all the help which might be needed, on the condition that it would be used for selfish purposes and only to the detriment of others. From the moment of signing a pact with the Devil, the more harm, misery, injury, and bitterness a man could inflict on those around him, the more help he could expect." Quite the musings from a ten year old! Just goes to show how extreme was his direct experience of the forces of evil.<br><br>If you are up for an unforgettable experience of terror expressed in the clear, vivid literary language of a fine writer, then you are ready for <i>The Painted Bird</i>.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1449264287i/17257934._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>‚ÄúThere's a place beyond words where experience first occurs to which I always want to return. I suspect that whenever I articulate my thoughts or translate my impulses into words, I am betraying the real thoughts and impulses which remain hidden.‚Äù<br>‚Äï Jerzy Kosinski, <i>The Painted Bird</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="15531342571351736492" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2117615546" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2117615546" />
    <a title="BlackOxford" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17744555-blackoxford"><img alt="BlackOxford" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1484863606p2/17744555.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2117615546?book_show_action=true">Oct 25, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="BlackOxford" class="user" itemprop="url" name="BlackOxford" href="/user/show/17744555-blackoxford">BlackOxford</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780553265200" href="/book/show/839806.The_Painted_Bird">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17744555-blackoxford?shelf=slavic">slavic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2117615546" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16102545627380730269"><b>The Real Spoils of War</b><br/><br/>In his <i>Being There</i>, Kosinski meditated on the consequences of being socialised entirely through television. <i>The Painted Bird</i> considers how a child might be socialised (if that doesn‚Äôt stretch the meaning of the word beyond its limits) to the chaos of war and the morally-deprived society in which it takes place. It‚Äôs not pretty.<br/><br/><a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[The unnamed protagonist loses contact with his parents at age six, and isn‚Äôt reunited with them until after he turns twelve. During th</span></span>
  <span id="freeText16102545627380730269" style="display:none"><b>The Real Spoils of War</b><br /><br />In his <i>Being There</i>, Kosinski meditated on the consequences of being socialised entirely through television. <i>The Painted Bird</i> considers how a child might be socialised (if that doesn‚Äôt stretch the meaning of the word beyond its limits) to the chaos of war and the morally-deprived society in which it takes place. It‚Äôs not pretty.<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The unnamed protagonist loses contact with his parents at age six, and isn‚Äôt reunited with them until after he turns twelve. During their separation, the boy is subject to the cruelty of the peasant society of rural Poland with its superstitious explanations of all natural phenomena, including the boy‚Äôs dark hair which makes him anathema as either a Gypsy or a Jew. He is also from time to time subject to equivalent cruelty by the invading German Army, not because he is either a Gypsy or a Jew but because he is an orphan with no obvious productive contribution to military efficiency.<br /><br />After a period of understandable confusion, the boy tries desperately to make sense of his new reality. His first attempts involve treating his situation in terms of some rational standard: if he works hard, keeps his mouth shut, and obeys, he reckons he should be safe. Of course, he isn‚Äôt. Cruelty increases without apparent cause or reason.<br /><br />Running away, the boy learns how to survive alone in a forest wilderness. But his isolation makes him vulnerable to capture by either the peasants or the Germans. Without communal protection he becomes doomed to a life of excruciating slavery. <br /><br />He then discovers what he thinks is both community and an explanation of his plight in the Christian religion. Prayer, he believes, is the answer to his suffering. If he can build enough credit with God, he will be delivered from injustice.<br /><br />But pray as he might nothing improves. In fact he is subject to extreme sexual abuse. He concludes not only that there is inherent, possibly irresistible, evil in the world but that the odds apparently favour those who side decisively with the Demon who embodies evil. This Manichaean turn may be distasteful but it appears the only way to achieve justice for his tormentors. Captured by evil, he becomes mute.<br /><br />His next epiphany occurs with the defeat of the Germans by the Soviets. He is taken in, cared for, and politically indoctrinated by soldiers of the Red Army. He sees what power is meant to be: protection of the weaker by the stronger. The fact that the reader may know of a dissonance between theory and practice in Soviet society is irrelevant to the boy‚Äôs experience. He perceives this new form of power as salvation.<br /><br />But salvation is only temporary. Placed in an orphanage with all the other mutilated and mentally damaged children of war, new skills of survival are required. Justice, in particular, cannot be left to social institutions, but must be seen to personally and proportionately to the offences involved. He becomes a street kid, a child of the night, a friend to low lifes and misfits. And he is comfortable.<br /><br />Until his parents appear. Suffering a kind of Stockholm Syndrome, the boy considers his estranged parents as an inconvenient constraint on his freedom and potential. His fantasies and grandiose dreams of success are threatened by parental supervision. His peace is shattered by the existence of an annoying younger step-brother. He is fundamentally unfit for family life.<br /><br />The rediscovery of his voice on the ski slopes of Switzerland is a rather ambiguous conclusion that the boy doesn‚Äôt comment upon. It‚Äôs unclear whether Kosinski is suggesting some sort of post-traumatic recovery or merely an explanation for how the first-person story is told at all. It is nevertheless clear that his life will never be normal in any sense of the word. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Postscript<br /><br />This article appeared in my 'feed' several days after I finished reading Kosinski. It is a re-interpretation of the Book of Job that is remarkably congruent with the thrust of Kosinski's narrative.<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.abc.net.au/religion/articles/2016/10/19/4559097.htm">http://www.abc.net.au/religion/articl...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="16102545627380730269" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2828104519" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2828104519" />
    <a title="Robin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36555254-robin"><img alt="Robin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1536187488p2/36555254.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2828104519?book_show_action=true">Jun 13, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robin" href="/user/show/36555254-robin">Robin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=poland">poland</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=2019">2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=debut">debut</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=semi-autobiographical">semi-autobiographical</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2828104519" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10780067654452521312">Recently, I said to a teacher of mine (as almost a point of pride) that I admire books that are void of sentimentality. He responded with, "Have you read Jerzy Kosinski?"<br/><br/>No, I hadn't. So I immediately sourced a copy of <i>The Painted Bird</i> thinking, sweet, giddy-up! I've read plenty of ruthless books. I'm no wilting daisy, no stranger to pages penned by an extra sharp quill.<br/><br/>But this book... this book is <b>hardcore</b>. <br/><br/>Based at least in part on the childhood experiences of the author, this 1965 novel foll</span>
  <span id="freeText10780067654452521312" style="display:none">Recently, I said to a teacher of mine (as almost a point of pride) that I admire books that are void of sentimentality. He responded with, "Have you read Jerzy Kosinski?"<br /><br />No, I hadn't. So I immediately sourced a copy of <i>The Painted Bird</i> thinking, sweet, giddy-up! I've read plenty of ruthless books. I'm no wilting daisy, no stranger to pages penned by an extra sharp quill.<br /><br />But this book... this book is <b>hardcore</b>. <br /><br />Based at least in part on the childhood experiences of the author, this 1965 novel follows the life of a 6 year old Polish boy who is sent away by his parents at the onset of WWII. He's meant to stay with a witchy peasant woman in a far away village, but that doesn't last for long. The boy is forced to survive in an unfriendly, rural area where he is despised by everyone who sees him because of the darkness of his hair and eyes. They don't know whether to hate him because he's a gypsy, or a Jew. Either way though, he is in trouble.<br /><br />Written in episodes, each chapter is a story of a new set of circumstances, a new horror show, a new twisted fairy tale, unlike anything you've read before. The child witnesses and endures the worst the world has to offer at the hands of the Polish peasantry. Often I had to put it aside to let my brain rest from the relentless pummelling it was taking, let the evil images have a chance to fade, before picking it up again.<br /><br />This book gives voice to the horrors of war, to be sure, and in particular the Nazi brand of horrors. But what struck me most about this scathing novel is its merciless illustration of the death of innocence. The main character starts off like any child, with the naive hope that he will be reunited with his parents and be returned to a safe, comfortable life. But with each episode, another hope is extinguished, another ideal is destroyed. This boy slowly loses faith in both God and people as he realises "every one of us stands alone". And how, given his experiences, can he come to any other conclusion?<br /><br />The title is taken from the chapter that features Lekh, a peasant who captures birds, and has the cruel habit of painting them in bright colours before setting them free. Then he watches as the flock, who can no longer recognise the bird as one of their own, viciously attacks and kills it. The boy is undoubtedly the painted bird, who cannot be seen as belonging anywhere he goes. He's left to a mixture of his own instincts and the random, careless luck of the world, in order to survive.<br /><br />I don't know that I would "recommend" this book to anyone, due to its extreme nature. I know that it has been criticised for exaggerating or exploiting the terrors of the Holocaust, as well as painting the Polish peasants as sadistic, ignorant filth. But personally, I have no problem believing that such horrendous things happened, and even worse, that the innocent hearts of children were hardened into existential lumps. I think it is of utmost importance that this controversial story be read by those who dare - to show us how close we all are to walking alone, and remind us to clutch tightly to each other and our humanity, above all else.<br /><br />Now, who's getting sentimental here??</span>
  <a data-text-id="10780067654452521312" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2512930124" />
    <a title="Violet wells" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4196681-violet-wells"><img alt="Violet wells" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442260743p2/4196681.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2512930124?book_show_action=true">Sep 03, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Violet wells" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Violet wells" href="/user/show/4196681-violet-wells">Violet wells</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2512930124" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18361616710443598866">I read in the introduction that Kosinski was attacked on several occasions by Polish nationals after publishing this novel. This because it's a merciless mockery and indictment of Polish Catholics and in particular the peasantry. In the 19th century literature tended to romanticise rural communities. A stance that rarely quite rings true nowadays. This novel perhaps exaggerates to the other extreme. However, one of the most memorable images in The Shoah documentary is the Polish farmer showing h</span>
  <span id="freeText18361616710443598866" style="display:none">I read in the introduction that Kosinski was attacked on several occasions by Polish nationals after publishing this novel. This because it's a merciless mockery and indictment of Polish Catholics and in particular the peasantry. In the 19th century literature tended to romanticise rural communities. A stance that rarely quite rings true nowadays. This novel perhaps exaggerates to the other extreme. However, one of the most memorable images in The Shoah documentary is the Polish farmer showing how he warned the Jews heading for Treblinka of their imminent fate. He makes a throat slitting gesture and then shows all his teeth in a wide grin. In that moment you realise he wasn't warning those Jews at all; he was mocking them. It's a sad fact that the populations of Germany's enemies often implemented Nazi policy much more comprehensively and enthusiastically than Nazi allies. Italy for example has a much better record than Poland (or France for that matter) for its behaviour towards Jews. By all accounts there was little racism in Italy. There are several cases of the Carabinieri destroying a population census in a district rather than handing it over to the Nazis. Italians hid Jews; there are virtually no cases of them betraying them for cash. I read recently that the Gestapo received tens of thousands of letters from French civilians denouncing Jews. Poland has perhaps the worst record of all. If you were a Jew in Poland, you had an enemy on the other side of virtually every wall. <br /><br /> I don't know any details of Kosinski's wartime experiences. Clearly they left him embittered and hating the country of his birth. However, he was a Polish Jew and survived so must have had help from Christian Poles. A subtext of this novel is the importance of formal education. The Polish peasantry are characterised as uncouth ignoramuses, medieval in their religious beliefs and philosophy. Witchcraft is still the prevailing belief system. <br /> <br /> But this is a novel, not a memoir, and should be evaluated as such. As a novel it has flaws, a couple of them fatal in my view. The Painted Bird is a very episodic novel. In fact, it's sometimes like the same short story written over and over again. This is certainly one flaw. There's also a sense that Kosinski gets carried away with his fascination for sadistic brutality. At times it reads like Holocaust porn, horror piled upon horror with a sense he's rather enjoying himself and inviting us to share his voyeuristic fascination. Less is more is a concept that completely escapes him. He piles on the atrocities, especially those of a sexual nature and it begins to make for very uncomfortable reading. Not because one is shocked; rather that one is embarrassed on the author's behalf by his obsessive fascination for depravity. This is particularly evident in a chapter when Ukrainian mercenaries terrorise a village. The acts these men subject the villagers to become almost comical in their escalating depravity. It's like the Marquis de Sade meets Monty Python. In fact you sense the novel's popularity was fed by all the perverted sex in its pages rather than as any kind of moving dramatization of the Holocaust. It's a shame because he writes very well and at times offers subtle and memorable insights into the dark side of human nature. Also, as an act of catharsis I dare say it provided benefits for him and you certainly can't begrudge him that. But it probably would have been a better novel had he waited until he was less avid to shock his readers. The Holocaust is shocking; we don't need anyone to exaggerate how shocking it was, even if with a mischievous ironical twinkle in the eye.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18361616710443598866" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3259227083" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3259227083" />
    <a title="Amalia Gkavea" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/56789747-amalia-gkavea"><img alt="Amalia Gkavea" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1559158764p2/56789747.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3259227083?book_show_action=true">Apr 02, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amalia Gkavea" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amalia Gkavea" href="/user/show/56789747-amalia-gkavea">Amalia Gkavea</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=1940s">1940s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=european-history">european-history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=european-literature">european-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=poland">poland</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=wwii-literature">wwii-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=polish-literature">polish-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3259227083" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6322370677857031580"><i>
  <b>''We are here in the company of death.''<br/> Jewish concentration camp inmate</b>
</i><br/><br/>A young boy finds himself lost, wandering in the countryside of an unnamed Eastern European country during the Second World War. The boy, mute and nameless, faces a world torn apart, a society that doesn't need any kind of war to change. It is a world stripped off all traces of kindness, compassion, and humanity, a world that preys upon a child in its most vulnerable moment.<br/><br/><i>
  <b>''As these brightly coloured creatures soug</b></i></span>
  <span id="freeText6322370677857031580" style="display:none"><i>
  <b>''We are here in the company of death.''<br /> Jewish concentration camp inmate</b>
</i><br /><br />A young boy finds himself lost, wandering in the countryside of an unnamed Eastern European country during the Second World War. The boy, mute and nameless, faces a world torn apart, a society that doesn't need any kind of war to change. It is a world stripped off all traces of kindness, compassion, and humanity, a world that preys upon a child in its most vulnerable moment.<br /><br /><i>
  <b>''As these brightly coloured creatures sought the safety of their fellows, the other birds, seeing them as threatening aliens, attacked and tore at the outcasts until they killed them''</b>
</i><br /><br />According to Kosi≈Ñski, this novel is a dark fable that follows the path of Aristophanes' <i>The Birds</i>. But ''dark'' doesn't even begin to describe it. I am sure most of you know have heard that <i>The Painted Bird</i> is cruelty personified. But it is a cruelty that needs to be read. Because it exposes every single monstrosity humans are capable of and we don't need war noises playing in the background to acknowledge this. We need to experience a boy's ordeals through birds and snakes and insects. A child who only vaguely recalls the tender moments of life with parents and whose personality is about to change by witnessing the most horrifying acts you can possibly think of.<br /><br />We need to witness Evil circling above the heads of villagers who, in this world, are the lowest form of life as seen by the young boy. Even the ravens and the vultures are disgusted by the human corpses. We have to experience the enchantment of the huldra, the cruelty against the Different, the mob that cries ''witchcraft'' while praying to a vicious Satan that no Bible could ever mention.<br /><br /><i>
  <b>''A rotting crucifix, once painted blue, stood at the crossroads. A holy picture hung at the top, from which a pair of barely visible but seemingly tear-stained eyes gazed into the empty fields and red glow of the rising sun. A gray bird sat on an arm of the cross. On catching sight of me, it spread its wings and vanished.''</b>
</i><br /><br /><i>
  <b>''I saw witches hanging from the trees. They stared at me, trying to lead me astray and confuse me. I distinctly heard the shudders of wandering souls which had escaped from the bodies of penitent sinners.''</b>
</i><br /><br />The boy's life is filled with graves in silent cemeteries and wandering skulls. The wind rages, the dead moan their sorrows, the dogs howl in desperation and madness. The prayers are hypocritical, the shadows are long and Death haunts the child's every step. The wrath of an animalistic mob knows no limits. There is no pity for the innocents who are jammed in the wagons, heading to the gas chambers according to the desires of the German viciousness of Hitler and his squad of monsters. They have no pity for those who try to escape their doom. There is no pity for a lonely child. He is a Jew. An Other. Cursed and vilified. Without a land, or a home, or a family. Without the right to exist. It doesn't matter who fights against the Nazis and the Soviets. The villagers need no pretext to unleash Hell. <br /><br />Yes, this novel contains every possible trigger warning you can think of. To the absolute extreme. And so does life itself. Do I think this portrayal is accurate? Do I believe that human nature is actually capable of the absolute Evil as portrayed in this novel? Of course, I do. The answer lies in the course of History.<br /><br /><i>
  <b>''The urge to survive in inherently unfettered. Can the imagination, any more than the boy, be held prisoner?''<br />Jerzy Kosi≈Ñski</b>
</i><br /><br />My reviews can also be found on <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://theopinionatedreaderblog.wordpress.com/">https://theopinionatedreaderblog.word...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="6322370677857031580" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <span id="freeText13031174047680009810" style="display:none">The story we find told by Kosinski, is nothing more than the reality of the evil experienced by hundreds of children and people during the Second World War. Violence and extreme brutality, but unfortunately they happened and lived in that period. The wickedness recounted in this story I never met in any other book read so far, where in a historical epoch the destiny of your life was dictated by belonging and physiognomy...<br>The extreme cruelty of the ancient popular beliefs in the rural world described here, is not something invented, but meandering and coexisting with the Christian faith.<br>What scandalizes here is the impossibility and total absence of a good and healthy tenderness, denied to all the hearts of history...<br>And here, however, I block and reject the idea that the author wants to give as written above.<br>I firmly believe that everywhere in the world there is always a pure man of heart, of a 'soul that loves you regardless....<br>I refuse to believe that man‚Äôs heart is always born fearful and kneeling to the world of evil and hatred.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1573535620i/28437753._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><br><br><br>La storia che troviamo narrata da Kosinski non √® altro che la realt√† del male vissuta da centinaia di bambini e persone durante la seconda guerra mondiale.   <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_758142593" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/758142593" />
    <a title="Bookcase Jim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10472582-bookcase-jim"><img alt="Bookcase Jim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1381662473p2/10472582.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/758142593?book_show_action=true">Nov 06, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bookcase Jim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bookcase Jim" href="/user/show/10472582-bookcase-jim">Bookcase Jim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer758142593" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer540994894857082974">After reading some of the reviews on here, I'm hoping that this will bring some sanity to the steaming heaps of hyperbole. Comparisons to the Saw films, torture porn, and complaints that the violence was simply all too gratuitous are the backbone of reviews that completely miss the point and should be dismissed out of hand. <br/><br/>"The purpose of a picaresque narrative is to present to the reader a picture of society and societal involvement that one would otherwise rather ignore, not all truths being </span>
  <span id="freeText540994894857082974" style="display:none">After reading some of the reviews on here, I'm hoping that this will bring some sanity to the steaming heaps of hyperbole. Comparisons to the Saw films, torture porn, and complaints that the violence was simply all too gratuitous are the backbone of reviews that completely miss the point and should be dismissed out of hand. <br /><br />"The purpose of a picaresque narrative is to present to the reader a picture of society and societal involvement that one would otherwise rather ignore, not all truths being pleasant ones." - Jerzy Kosinski's <i>The Painted Bird</i> and the Picaresque Tradition, Joseph V. Ricapito, in Neohelicon (1977) <br /><br />I strongly recommend reading the book from the very beginning; Start by reading Jerzy Kosinski's foreword. He discusses its reception, some of his motives for writing it, and the background to the story. He explains that this is fiction, he also explains, as he does in the pages of the novel, the phenomenon/tradition of a painted bird. This is the key motif to the story. Missing the significance here is missing the point entirely. As I mentioned above, most reviewers of this novel read it without even understanding it. He also says it was not an autobiographical account, so again, I don't see why reviewers on here have to bring up the controversy surrounding 'facts' in his <i>fictional</i> novel. <br /><br /><br />That being said, Kosinski also mentions that, amidst the overwhelming criticism from his native Poland, where it was banned, some of the book's supporters were critical of his 'watered down' depiction of events, given that many occurrences that took place in Europe during the war were much more dramatic and cruel than those described in <i>The Painted Bird</i>. I tend to believe this; art imitates life, not the other way around. <br /><br />The novel follows a young city boy who is sent out to the countryside to stay away from the war. Unfortunately his parents didn't realize that the countryside is rife with primitive, illiterate peasants. Or maybe they did, but figured they are a better alternative to the invading Nazis. The trouble with this little boy is that he's got a darker complexion than the fair skinned, blond haired natives of the villages. Wherever he goes, he is immediately branded as the worst thing to be; a gypsy, or Jew (or both). In either case, the superstitious villagers despise and fear him (for his supposed evil powers). And then bad things happen to him.<br /><br />At one time in that part of the world, probably for entertainment, people would sometimes capture a bird and paint vivid colours over its feathers. They would then release the bird who flew straight back to its own. The flock, confused by the stranger's appearance, would begin to attack the painted bird and eventually kill it, not recognizing it as one of their own. The painted bird, in turn, would likely not understand why its feathery friends were trying to kill it. <br /><br />The allegory Kosinski makes is very simple in my eyes. <br /><br />The boy, a living, breathing human being, is not recognized as one of their own by confused and uneducated peasants. Each beating and humiliating act that he endures is a violent peck against his outward appearance. Simple. <br />The novel is called <i>The Painted Bird</i>. Does anyone really believe it's just a quirky title related to the tradition of painting a bird's feathers?<br /><br />Then there were some things that just somehow made it difficult to take seriously. It often reads like a grocery list of superstitions and neo-pagan beliefs. Recipes for simple ailments that involve ground bones, horse teeth, and various secretions. I found the description of these potions more disgusting than the violence for the most part. The superstitions were everywhere though:<br />- The belief that gypsies have the power to kill by looking in one's eyes. <br />- The need to spit three time in any gypsies' direction to counter the 'death-look'. <br />- Hiding/covering one's teeth when smiling in case a gypsy might count them (and take that amount of years off your life). <br />- The myriad of demons and spirits who roam the forests and the fields. <br />- The rope of a suicidee that's meant to bring good luck. <br /><br />These, and many many more, became fact by the end of book. They certainly became fact for our anti-hero who himself came to believe the very same things. <br /><br />Kosinsky also describes some situations that read as if straight out of Borat. The Polish peasant is made to look stupider and more ignorant than any other being on the planet. From this point of view, I'm not surprised it was banned in commie Poland. <br /><br />Finally, I'm going to add a personal note. I grew up in a place where corporal punishment was still doled out at school. I had strangers who were very mean to me as kid, when I was the same age as Kosinsky's 'painted bird'. I had my hair and ears pulled undeservedly by adults. I went to school with kids who'd clearly been badly beaten at home. I had the back of my hands smacked by the teacher's ruler. I was slapped by a doctor once for crying. In my eyes, adults were large creatures to be feared. They loved to scare and hurt kids. They were mean, cruel people. And this was in the 80s, in a city, and I didn't look different from anybody else. <br /><br />Yes it‚Äôs unbelievable that one child could suffer so much to our coddled Western minds, but to think that a child at the mercy of a bunch of uneducated, backwater type, Eastern European rednecks, during the blood orgy that was the Second World War, would not have to endure much much worse is naive. I too believe that given the setting and circumstances, Kosinski offers a watered-down narrative. <br /><br />I can't give this five stars because I think Kosinski could've gone further with the material and subject matter at hand. Both from the perspective of the narrative and from a literary point of view. It was, at times, too two-dimensional, and I breezed right through it. Although that's the point of the picaresque novel it's hard not to ask for more. I enjoyed it for the historical context very much, and for an accurate reflection of our human condition. <br />Maybe I just wish he'd shown it as a painting instead of a caricature. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="540994894857082974" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText1284702942747765284" style="display:none"><b>The officer surveyed me sharply. I felt like a squashed caterpillar oozing in the dust, a creature that could not harm anyone yet aroused loathing and disgust. In the presence of such a resplendent being, armed in all the symbols of might and majesty, I was genuinely ashamed of my appearance. <div id="review_1838245825" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1838245825" />
    <a title="Nate D" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/406701-nate-d"><img alt="Nate D" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1203897989p2/406701.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1838245825?book_show_action=true">Dec 13, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nate D" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nate D" href="/user/show/406701-nate-d">Nate D</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=poland">poland</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=read-in-2016">read-in-2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=60s-re-de-construction">60s-re-de-construction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=wwii">wwii</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1838245825" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14416918789037681308">The undiminished, unending nightmarish brutality of this novel might become an exercise in repetition and gratuity, were it not for undiminished horror contained in its subjects, taken both narrowly (the holocaust) and more broadly (the bottomless capacity of humans to inflict atrocities upon other humans). Early phantasmagoric and folkloric aspects and presentation as a series of episodic 'fables' creates an expectation of the hope/fear rhythms of a fairytale, but the first side of that equatio</span>
  <span id="freeText14416918789037681308" style="display:none">The undiminished, unending nightmarish brutality of this novel might become an exercise in repetition and gratuity, were it not for undiminished horror contained in its subjects, taken both narrowly (the holocaust) and more broadly (the bottomless capacity of humans to inflict atrocities upon other humans). Early phantasmagoric and folkloric aspects and presentation as a series of episodic 'fables' creates an expectation of the hope/fear rhythms of a fairytale, but the first side of that equation, instead, is rigorously denied, along with any chance of reading pleasure. That said, it's an impressive novel, particularly in how the horror extends into the post-war peace -- for this child protagonist, there will be no return to pre-war wholeness or stability.<br /><br />I was reading some other reviews of this one, particularly Paul Bryant's where he outlines the book's failure to be credible despite its impressive force. I'm not sure how the book was received when it appeared (besides by Kosinki's accounts of being attacked for it by people who took it very very literally) but I don't even think realism is the intent here. This is inconceivably the true experience of any one character. Instead, Kosiniski seems to be drawing from the undeniable factual devastation and cruelty of the period to construct a horrific symbolic widescreen tableaux of people acting horribly towards eachother (this obviously extends well beyond the acts specifically directed at the protagonist throughout), in Poland or elsewhere. In this aim, it seems to capture something all too disquietingly real.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14416918789037681308" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_613260389" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/613260389" />
    <a title="Yuri Kruman" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10966884-yuri-kruman"><img alt="Yuri Kruman" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1452707947p2/10966884.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/613260389?book_show_action=true">May 11, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Yuri Kruman" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Yuri Kruman" href="/user/show/10966884-yuri-kruman">Yuri Kruman</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer613260389" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17930305252873341047">Let fools argue about Kosi≈Ñski's biography and "authenticity" of experience. His supreme ability to tell a child's horrific coming-of-age story in rural Nazi-controlled Poland, where the peasants are just as gruesomely sadistic, with adult credibility and moral authority without overreaching or sentimentality, is a dark and bittersweet triumph of humanity and then also of literature. <br/><br/>IMHO, the book was not written as an invective against Polish peasants or Nazis alone, any more than it is solely</span>
  <span id="freeText17930305252873341047" style="display:none">Let fools argue about Kosi≈Ñski's biography and "authenticity" of experience. His supreme ability to tell a child's horrific coming-of-age story in rural Nazi-controlled Poland, where the peasants are just as gruesomely sadistic, with adult credibility and moral authority without overreaching or sentimentality, is a dark and bittersweet triumph of humanity and then also of literature. <br /><br />IMHO, the book was not written as an invective against Polish peasants or Nazis alone, any more than it is solely an indictment of human nature in wartime. Kosi≈Ñski's scalpel cut too deep to the marrow to stop at Mitka-style revenge. His truth lies deeper than in paranoia and suspicion of man; it exposes the harrowing duality of man's nature, sharpened to extremes in wartime - by turns cruel and tender, superstitious and rational, vengeful and forgiving, brutal and sophisticated. <br /><br />The Painted Bird is the strongest, most harrowing novel I've ever read and it will stay with me for life, both as a Jew, human being and as a writer. It is an incredibly difficult and important book, and the fact of the controversy surrounding it and the author only heightens its effect.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17930305252873341047" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83445-lisa-vegan?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83445-lisa-vegan?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83445-lisa-vegan?shelf=z1983">z1983</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2790979" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer383835372696212317">I read an earlier edition than this. I‚Äôve read many, many holocaust era books and I‚Äôd already read quite a few when I read this one. And this says a lot, but this one might be the most horrifying one of them all. This was one of the most disturbing books I‚Äôve ever read and yet I loved the book. I read it thinking it was non-fiction; years after I read it, I read that it was fiction, but that doesn‚Äôt diminish at all the impact I feel from reading it. It‚Äôs truly amazing what people can do to each </span>
  <span id="freeText383835372696212317" style="display:none">I read an earlier edition than this. I‚Äôve read many, many holocaust era books and I‚Äôd already read quite a few when I read this one. And this says a lot, but this one might be the most horrifying one of them all. This was one of the most disturbing books I‚Äôve ever read and yet I loved the book. I read it thinking it was non-fiction; years after I read it, I read that it was fiction, but that doesn‚Äôt diminish at all the impact I feel from reading it. It‚Äôs truly amazing what people can do to each other and also what people will go through in order to survive. Do recommend it but only for those with their own courage to plough through such a horrific tale.</span>

    <a title="Kim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/520753-kim"><img alt="Kim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1402541317p2/520753.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/52365623?book_show_action=true">Apr 12, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kim" href="/user/show/520753-kim">Kim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer52365623" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer17166454224680980701">This review is serving as a spiritual tug of war. The Battle of the Conscious. I really don‚Äôt know what to think. I hesitate between 2 and 3 stars and Yeah, I know‚Ä¶ I‚Äôm a heartless bitch. The guilt tells me to rate it higher because of all the persecution and just plain ol‚Äô <b>H</b>orrors that this kid dealt with. As if I‚Äôve lost some humanity if I don‚Äôt appreciate this more. But‚Ä¶ another part of me is just not feeling it. <br/><br/>It sort of feels like rubbernecking. <br/><br/>Like, it starts off right away with explodi</span>
  <span id="freeText17166454224680980701" style="display:none">This review is serving as a spiritual tug of war. The Battle of the Conscious. I really don‚Äôt know what to think. I hesitate between 2 and 3 stars and Yeah, I know‚Ä¶ I‚Äôm a heartless bitch. The guilt tells me to rate it higher because of all the persecution and just plain ol‚Äô <b>H</b>orrors that this kid dealt with. As if I‚Äôve lost some humanity if I don‚Äôt appreciate this more. But‚Ä¶ another part of me is just not feeling it. <br /><br />It sort of feels like rubbernecking. <br /><br />Like, it starts off right away with exploding pet squirrels and just gets more and more unbelievable as you go. I‚Äôm not saying that this stuff couldn‚Äôt have happened (well, maybe I am, but not to ONE person...)and I am well aware that as a French Canadian child born in 1970‚Äôs New England I would have NO BASIS on which to judge the monstrosities that this un-named child endured. But, it‚Äôs not <i>really</i> real, you know? No? Okay, then I‚Äôm colder than I thought. <br /><br />I can‚Äôt get past the fact that this kid really isn‚Äôt ever given a break‚Ä¶ We go through about 200 pages of beatings and rapes and mutilations and near deaths and you (me) start to roll your eyes and think that Charlie Brown could take some life lessons from this little gypsy boy. <br /><br />I felt guilty! I shouldn‚Äôt be exasperated by this! I should be appalled! I should be weeping and thanking unknown deities that my lame childhood was all about having to walk to McDonalds and clean the pool. But, what did I do? I started skimming. Blah-Blah-beating-Blah-Blah-hanging from a hook to avoid certain death from vicious dog-blah blah-skinning a live rabbit-blah blah-thrown in a dung heap---Blah Blah---you get the picture. <br /><br />The only thread of compassion that I hold onto is that I didn‚Äôt feel this way when I read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/48855.Anne_Frank_The_Diary_of_a_Young_Girl" title="Anne Frank The Diary of a Young Girl by Anne Frank" rel="nofollow noopener">Anne Frank The Diary of a Young Girl</a> or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1617.Night" title="Night by Elie Wiesel" rel="nofollow noopener">Night</a>. I‚Äôm not a monster. (Right?) <br /><br />Maybe it was the writing style, the dispassionate, removed, telling of these events. Then interspersed with these flowing images of painted birds and sexual awakenings (rape or not.) It was difficult to juxtapose between these styles and stay in the moment. My breath would catch at a particularly moving passage like:<br /><br /><i>I had seen the end of terror that shakes one until it squeezes the stomach empty of vomit, like a punctured poppy pod blown open by the wind.</i><br /><br />Then move a few paragraphs to:<br /><br /><i>I began to practice walking. My legs did not obey me and I tired easily. One night I heard noises outside. I peered through a slot between the boards. </i><br /><br />You lose the rhythm, the fantasy, and hit the mundane again. My addled brain couldn‚Äôt handle it. <br /><br />I read that Kosinski may have gone all <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/822.James_Frey" title="James Frey" rel="nofollow noopener">James Frey</a> with this and ‚Äòembellished‚Äô the tale but from what I‚Äôve read, he never really said it wasn‚Äôt fiction, right? I mean, <i>others</i> fought to find meaning and truth behind the tale. Then there‚Äôs the controversy surrounding plagiarism and then all those health issues he had‚Ä¶ Poor guy, no wonder he committed suicide. (See? Compassion!)<br /><br />So, I‚Äôm glad that I read this. I have <i>Being There</i> and I‚Äôve been wanting to read that for awhile‚Ä¶ not realizing it was the same author. Only now, I‚Äôm not so excited. <br />And that‚Äôs sad. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17166454224680980701" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
