    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1252732377" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1252732377" />
    <a title="Selkie ‚ú¶ Queen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28165536-selkie-queen"><img alt="Selkie ‚ú¶ Queen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1587803822p2/28165536.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1252732377?book_show_action=true">Apr 12, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Selkie ‚ú¶ Queen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Selkie ‚ú¶ Queen" href="/user/show/28165536-selkie-queen">Selkie ‚ú¶ Queen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28165536-selkie-queen?shelf=book-diet-2015-2016">book-diet-2015-2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28165536-selkie-queen?shelf=classic-contemporary">classic-contemporary</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1252732377" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1594970261484819940">On deciding for the title of this novel, writer Jerzy Kosinki was inspired by the symbolic use of birds in literature which "allowed certain people to deal with actual events and characters without the restrictions which the writing of history imposes". He states that there was a certain peasant custom he witnessed as a child before in which he describes it as follows:<br/><br/><blockquote>
  <i>"One of the villagers' favorite entertainment was trapping birds, painting their feathers, and then releasing them in the air </i></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText1594970261484819940" style="display:none">On deciding for the title of this novel, writer Jerzy Kosinki was inspired by the symbolic use of birds in literature which "allowed certain people to deal with actual events and characters without the restrictions which the writing of history imposes". He states that there was a certain peasant custom he witnessed as a child before in which he describes it as follows:<br><br><blockquote>
  <i>"One of the villagers' favorite entertainment was trapping birds, painting their feathers, and then releasing them in the air to rejoin their flock. As these brightly colored creatures sought the safety of their fellows, <b>the other birds, seeing them as threatening aliens, attacked and tore at the outcasts until they killed them."</b></i>
</blockquote> <br>Due to the controversial nature and content of this book, I was surprised that I even stumbled upon a copy about a year ago while once again casually flipping through the general section of a bookstore. I've only known about the book months prior to acquiring it, and I was so excited to start reading it on a scheduled time. Some months later, I did just that and for two days I was immersed in witnessing the ugliest and most vile horrors I have ever read in fiction that were loosely based from real-life accounts of people who lived through the second World War. There was nothing about this book I enjoyed, to be honest. It was psychologically painful and slightly numbing to peruse through, especially with each chapter dealing with deprived deviant acts of the social and sexual kind. <br><br>That being said, this is a spectacular novel that examines the darker and sickening aspects of human nature, and it was successful in its depiction because I don't think any decent person would enjoy the varying degrees of cruelty and degradation that Kosinski have shared in <i>The Painted Bird</i>.<br><br><i>The Painted Bird</i> follows the travels of a six-year-old Jewish boy in Central Europe, and whose parents have sent him away in beliefs that he would fare better away from the heart of the warfare and Nazism at the time. <br><br>What happens mainly instead over the course of the book is that the boy was forced to grow up very quickly, robbed of another option, as he stays in one village after another, more often discriminated against, beaten up and rarely cared for. As a book that deals with the Holocaust, Kosinski managed to stay away from events surrounding the actual prison or labor camps where the Jews were gassed or incinerated. We all know that's where the real horror lies but Kosinski challenged this idea and revealed to us that in times of warfare, even the most modest of places such as rural villages can be sources of the most potent evil human beings are capable of. <br><br>This book delved deeply on the shocking ways that antisemitic sentiment, religious persecution and barbaric superstitions could turn people into hateful creatures; that even the simple folk back then can and will ruefully participate in terrible acts, often justifying their malicious intentions as divine interventions, against the boy himself, and any Jewish or Gypsy person of the same ilk who would pass their way. I don't think it's worth specifying these truly disgusting and abhorrent events here in my review, mostly because I'm still sick to my stomach just thinking about them. Even the subtlest ways of these people when it comes to their maltreatment of the boy just because he has black hair and dark eyes (and therefore an abomination to God) were chilling in retrospect. <br><br>So, yes, I did not enjoy reading this book but I was fully hypnotized into trudging along each chapter anyway.<br><br>I could then claim that this was a great exercise on moral conscience and inherent human compassion on the end of the readers such as myself who have developed a certain keen sense of cynicism over the years regarding the world at large. I am not shocked easily by gory details but I have to admit that this book made me feel bad every time I try to insert some humor in my initial thoughts in Goodreads for the reading updates while reading. It doesn't feel like a subject to be made light of, personally, but it was also the only way I can endure reading the chapters--I had to find some sort of morbid amusement and detachment just so I don't get thoroughly disheartened. <br><br>What was so moving about this novel, however, was the main character of the boy who remains unnamed throughout, but whose iron will and resilient youth had made it possible for him to come out on the other side alive, though fragmented and forever changed. Children are tougher than we give them credit for, and I was comforted with the fact that he was resourceful in adapting to multiple situations where his own life and innocence are fully at stake. This book features tons of examples of mob mentality (the likes of which are awfully symbolized by the painted-birds analogy Kosinski has utilized), as well as separate incidents of incest and bestiality, and a rather disconcerting abundance of gang rapes at the later part of the book where a whole chapter is devoted describing the entire thing in painstakingly gross detail. This is not a book meant for enjoyment so if you happen to decide you want to read it, please remember what I just said in this review.<br><br><i>The Painted Bird</i> also operates on the wisdom that there are no happy lives, just happy moments, and about fifty pages near the end, the readers are allowed to view snapshots of the boy's life in the aftermath of the fall of the Third Reich and though there was nothing immediately uplifting about it, it's the best happy ending he could make out of from the traumatic experiences that have shaped him, and malformed him somehow. <br><br>Personally, I didn't expect that there's going to be a healing message by the end of this tragic tale anyway. I think the ambiguity of the resolution for <i>The Painted Bird</i> accomplishes what it was set out to do in the first place: to remind readers that the darkness hovering in our lives is real and it could seep through the cracks, whether or not we allow it. <br><br>But the real test of courage and spiritual enlightenment is on how we cope and deal with the poison that corrodes our systems, and I would like to believe against hope that we can rise above our own base impulses towards hatred, ignorance and persecution. There is corruption and sickness in the world, yes, but we all should strive to be the balm on its infected pores. <br><br><i>The Painted Bird</i>, after showing me so much inhumane and malicious acts that people do to each other, has also reminded me of my humanity and the blessings and burdens of ensuring I don't give in to the call of moral decay and disintegration of values, no matter how easy (and even remotely tempting) it is to act like lesser beings.<br><br><b>RECOMMENDED: 9/10<br><br>DO READ MY REVIEWS AT<br><br><a href="http://reademption-lit.blogspot.com/" rel="nofollow noopener"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1416741261i/12051318.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></a></b></span>
  <a data-text-id="1594970261484819940" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_149887462" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/149887462" />
    <a title="Kathryn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1497350-kathryn"><img alt="Kathryn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1250569584p2/1497350.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/149887462?book_show_action=true">Feb 22, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kathryn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kathryn" href="/user/show/1497350-kathryn">Kathryn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1497350-kathryn?shelf=war">war</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1497350-kathryn?shelf=gruesome-horror">gruesome-horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1497350-kathryn?shelf=historical">historical</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer149887462" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7246386878101739299">The night before last, I fell asleep holding my laptop, while on the couch. I could have sworn I had saved my work, which just so happened to be a review for this book, but upon logging onto Goodreads today I noticed that I did not have a review for <i>The Painted Bird</i>. Every time I am certain I have everything planned out in terms of writing and posting reviews, I do something stupid, such as falling asleep with my computer. Sadly, falling asleep while contemplating and discussing a book does not </span>
  <span id="freeText7246386878101739299" style="display:none">The night before last, I fell asleep holding my laptop, while on the couch. I could have sworn I had saved my work, which just so happened to be a review for this book, but upon logging onto Goodreads today I noticed that I did not have a review for <i>The Painted Bird</i>. Every time I am certain I have everything planned out in terms of writing and posting reviews, I do something stupid, such as falling asleep with my computer. Sadly, falling asleep while contemplating and discussing a book does not bode well for my opinion of the book but before you read any further, I by no means wish to discourage anyone from picking up this one of a kind novel.<br /><br />I was bored at times, becoming lost in the extreme violence, graphic descriptions of torture and sexual acts, and in what semblence of story progression that existed. I also experienced huge problems connecting with the main character, a six-year-old boy. I have a six-year-old boy, so I like to think I have a basic understanding of the average developmental stage for the age, which not mesh with this book. I found it difficult to imagine the main character in such a setting and that the majority of the people he met were only interested in hurting him. This novel is not about a bad case of indifference, as is most often found in books about World War II and the Holocaust. This book is about hate and sadism, about evil born out of poverty and ignorance. Maybe this reflects more about me than the book, but I find it impossible to believe that the main character could encounter so much active and persistent evil. Maybe if the book had followed a young man or adult, the story would be more believable, barely. I just have this inclination to believe that people are less likely to harm a child. This book is saying that because he was a child, he was harmed more. His age, naivety, and innocence certainly <i>allowed</i> people increased opportunities to prey upon him but I just can not believe that these qualities <i>encouraged</i> the abuse on the scale found in this story.<br /><br /><i>The Painted Bird</i> was ridiculous, at times, in its extremes. If half of the atrocities had been edited out, then I would have found the story much more compelling and terrifying. Please excuse the horrible pun, but the entire novel felt like overkill. Yet maybe this was the author's intention. Holding back on the amount of evil was not a characteristic of events of this period in history. So maybe I am considering this novel from too much of a literal perspective. The fact that I am unsure of the author's intentions bothers me. I tend to be unconcerned if my interpretation of a book is backwards from the intentions of the author. Once a book is published, the equivalent of releasing the work into the wild, an author must be open to personal interpretation but this book does not really allow for that in my opinion. The story is straightforward and predictable and full of pointless violence, at times. Is the author commenting on the effect of immense abuse? On the will to survive? Xenophobia? Does the author provide any cause and effect? Do we see how the events in this book have shaped the boy? <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[We do, slightly, towards the very end. This was not enough for me <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. <br /><br />I feel a good historical novel should scream the whole bit about those who forget history are doomed to repeat it. This book did no such thing. It more or less screamed <b>abuse!, molestation!, torture!, assault!, murder!, bestiality!, war!,....come and find it within these pages!</b>....on and on and on, rather akin to a circus act. Every physical hurt you can concieve of, with little mention of the mental and emotional backlash. I guess the reader is meant to understand that the inner emotional pain exists, just not enough for me. There was a bit involving the contemplation of good, evil, religion, and lack of faith, and though I really liked these parts of the book, I still feel that the inner workings of the boy's mind were glossed over.<br /> <br />The book tends to focus on sensationalistic violence. There is zero dialogue, something I did not notice at first but bothered me once I did. I did love how the author worked in the superstitious and local beliefs, especially concerning Gypsies and traditional remedies. The book felt as more of a manual, full of instructions on how-to-become-a-successful-witch-by-casting-curses-while-traveling-through-the-Polish-countryside. I also immensely enjoyed the Red Army scenes. All of the historical aspects of the book were quite entertaining.<br /><br />Learning about the author has also been interesting. He was accused of plagiarism, which always sparks my interest for some odd reason, and he was not a favorite of the critics, which endears him to me more than not. He seemed to be an immensely intelligent man and his suicide the fodder of cult followings. I agree with other reviews mentioning the lack of sophistication of the writing in <i>The Painted Bird</i>, largely due to the predominantly juvenile tone. It was not until the last few pages that I realized the author meant to do this, maybe an attempt to allow readers to better connect with the main character. I really do not care if this is the case, as it did not work, and I read the entire book wondering why the writing felt overly simple. Realizing at the end that it was intentional and that I am too simple to pick up on the fact does not improve my feelings for the book. These types of story qualities should be effortless and conveyed seemlessly, without the reader even noticing. I want to become lost in my books, not Miss Analytical.<br /><br />When I enter the stage of making excuses for a book, of trying to explain the <i>whys</i> with many <i>what-ifs</i> and/or <i>probablies</i>, then it tends to reflect an unsatisfied overall feeling. A seemless and stellar read means that I become so very lost that I do not notice anything about the book, nothing at all, except for what it makes me feel. A book about the things I've already mentioned should have me sobbing. I generally do not read Holocaust books as I can not tolerate the imagery but I did not develop enough of a connection to warrant massive reactions with this book. There was one scene that nearly did me in but it was not about the main character. It was about another boy. I set the book aside for nearly a week after this scene but once I returned, I wanted the entire thing to end. Even after all of my comments, I will still probably read more by this author. I have heard that his voice changes rather dramatically from book to book, so maybe a second attempt is worth my time. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7246386878101739299" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText9819933873774763123" style="display:none">The Painted Bird had a profound effect on me as a teen (and I don't care about all the controversy surrounding the life of the author Jerzy Kosi≈Ñski). It was intense and shocking to read about an orphaned Jewish boy (not much younger than myself at the time of reading) on the run and trying to avoid being captured or killed in WW2 Europe. <br /><br />Savage. Bleak helplessness. But also the feeling that sometimes, unfortunately, "this is real life"...<br /><br />I think somehow humanity needs to understand the nature of evil to evolve. Where does evil come from? And why is it so prevalent? If you're interested in such questions, then The Painted Bird is for you.<br /><br />Who else is brave enough to watch the upcoming cinema adaptation of the story? <br />It's been called the most controversial movie of the year and some critics even think it should be banned...but it's really the only film I am wanting to watch at the moment.<br /><br />The Painted Bird (2019) trailer: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=cH5YKki0KBs">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=cH5YK...</a><br /><br />The Painted Bird IMDb: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.imdb.com/title/tt1667354/">https://www.imdb.com/title/tt1667354/</a><br /><br />Holocaust drama 'The Painted Bird' prompts mass walkout at Toronto Film Festival <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.yahoo.com/entertainment/holocaust-drama-the-painted-bird-prompts-mass-walkout-at-toronto-film-festival-113003890.html">https://www.yahoo.com/entertainment/h...</a></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer842219974" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2211945752119320734">It was clear from the beginning this story was not going to be on the light side, and being a reader who typically tends toward darker material, that did not deter me in any way from launching into this book. Not even sure from whence it came, but it has been languishing in one of our bookcases for years. I almost wish I had left it there, unread. At my age, I am well aware of the inequities of life. <br/><br/>The atrocities seen by and committed to the little boy in the book are almost continual, before </span>
  <span id="freeText2211945752119320734" style="display:none">It was clear from the beginning this story was not going to be on the light side, and being a reader who typically tends toward darker material, that did not deter me in any way from launching into this book. Not even sure from whence it came, but it has been languishing in one of our bookcases for years. I almost wish I had left it there, unread. At my age, I am well aware of the inequities of life. <br /><br />The atrocities seen by and committed to the little boy in the book are almost continual, before it was over he was rendered mute. His only means of defense was to do his best not to be noticed. It was rarely successful. As I read page after page of beatings, torture, bestiality, rape, and murder I found myself almost becoming inured to whatever horror was going to come up next. <br /><br />I did find the superstitions of the Slavic peasants to be fascinating, but I would think long and hard before recommending this book. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Philippe" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Philippe" href="/user/show/4903281-philippe">Philippe</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780553265200" href="/book/show/839806.The_Painted_Bird">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4903281-philippe?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4903281-philippe?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1094189824" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer719573117195195675">It was a high school teacher who first acquainted me with this book. The Painted Bird really hit me when I read it as an adolescent. The afterglow of this crescendo of violence stayed with me for a long time. Rereading the book after a few decades I better see its flaws. There is something two-dimensional and contrived about it that an experienced reader will flinch from. Still there are scenes which are difficult to put out of one‚Äôs mind (but now I would situate them earlier in the story rather</span>
  <span id="freeText719573117195195675" style="display:none">It was a high school teacher who first acquainted me with this book. The Painted Bird really hit me when I read it as an adolescent. The afterglow of this crescendo of violence stayed with me for a long time. Rereading the book after a few decades I better see its flaws. There is something two-dimensional and contrived about it that an experienced reader will flinch from. Still there are scenes which are difficult to put out of one‚Äôs mind (but now I would situate them earlier in the story rather than later). And the book‚Äôs narrative nucleus - homo homini lupus, amplified by the blind forces of war and superstition - strikes me as authentic. And not at all fanciful considering that today on television we see 12-year old boys brandishing severed heads. Finally, what will also stay with me is the role of the landscape in this tale. The faceless Eastern European expanses are a shadow protagonist. Uncannily they reflect and amplify the terror experienced by the lead character. <br /><br />I probably won‚Äôt be rereading this. But if any of our children ask me whether this is worth picking up I will recommend it, with the appropriate caveats. </span>
  <a data-text-id="719573117195195675" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1823648299" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1823648299" />
    <a title="Ms.pegasus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5015851-ms-pegasus"><img alt="Ms.pegasus" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1335705124p2/5015851.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1823648299?book_show_action=true">Nov 27, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ms.pegasus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ms.pegasus" href="/user/show/5015851-ms-pegasus">Ms.pegasus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/10724147-the-painted-bird">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5015851-ms-pegasus?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5015851-ms-pegasus?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1823648299" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4408357784082354706">The darkness of this book is tangible and unmitigated. The narrative voice is that of a six year old boy living in Poland at the onset of World War II. By the conclusion of the war, Kosinski has stripped every vestige of innocence from this boy, and extinguished any faith the reader might have had in humanity. <br/><br/>The boy is dark haired and olive skinned. An alien in the villages of the fair-haired. He speaks a different language, or at least a strange dialect. Wherever he wanders, peasants either s</span>
  <span id="freeText4408357784082354706" style="display:none">The darkness of this book is tangible and unmitigated. The narrative voice is that of a six year old boy living in Poland at the onset of World War II. By the conclusion of the war, Kosinski has stripped every vestige of innocence from this boy, and extinguished any faith the reader might have had in humanity. <br /><br />The boy is dark haired and olive skinned. An alien in the villages of the fair-haired. He speaks a different language, or at least a strange dialect. Wherever he wanders, peasants either shun him, fear him, or capture and abuse him. Lurid superstitions about evil gypsies and malignant Jews were alive in these isolated villages even before the Nazis unleashed their official policy of genocide. <br /><br />The title is a revolting indication of what will unfold. To amuse themselves, peasants would capture a bird and paint its feathers. The garishly arrayed bird would then be released. Exhilarated by its freedom, the bird would rise to rejoin its flock. There, its former companions would attack the painted bird and peck it do death. The peasants would watch as the painted, blood-covered corpse plummeted to the ground. <br /><br />Kosinski, the most disciplined of writers, never lets up. He begins with subtle parallels. A pigeon tries in vain to join a flock of hens. When a hawk swoops down, the flock leaves the pigeon isolated, and it becomes easy prey. The boy befriends a squirrel in the forest only to watch helplessly as a band of boys capture the squirrel and set fire to it. Another gang of children tosses him into an icy river and laughs as he floats away, clinging to a large fish bladder. The atrocities are not limited to rejections of the outsider. A jealous husband gouges out the eyeballs of a young farmhand as the boy watches. A crowd of women viciously beats and stones to death a character named Stupid Ludmila, mentally damaged from being raped and beaten. Kosinski recounts incident after incident with horrifying intensity. In each case, the impressionable boy is a helpless observer of the suffering victim. The reader too is helpless ‚Äî a helpless witness to these uninterrupted scenes of brutality and to the demise of the impressionable boy's innocence.<br /><br />He perceives the adult indifference to sadistic brutality. He moves through a progression of conclusions. A witchy folk healer named Olga shelters him. <i> ‚ÄúFrom her I learned for the first time that I was possessed by an evil spirit, which crouched in me like a mole in a deep burrow, and of whose presence I was unaware.‚Äù</i> (Location 482). He struggles to make sense of the brutality he sees. The aura of magic surrounding the rituals of the Catholic Church suggests prayer will provoke God's intervention. <i> ‚ÄúSuddenly the ruling pattern of the world was revealed to me with beautiful clarity. I understood why some people were strong and others weak, some free and others enslaved, some rich and others poor, some well and others sick. The former had simply been the first to see the need for prayer and for collecting the maximum number of days of indulgence‚Äù</i> (Location 1823) Magic and power are his sole tools for constructing understanding. Later, the boy concludes <i> ‚ÄúWhat mattered was that a man should consciously promote evil, find pleasure in harming others, nurturing and using the diabolical powers granted him by the Evil Ones in a manner calculated to cause as much misery and suffering around him as possible.‚Äù</i> (Location 2168) Certainly the Germans had embraced evil; this was the explanation for their power. When, finally, the Russian Army advances and he is taken in by a soldier named Gavrila, he learns: <i> ‚ÄúIn this world there were realistic ways of promoting goodness, and there were people who had dedicated their whole lives to it. These were the Communist Party members.‚Äù</i> (Location 2664) Conveniently, his new mentors are unaware of Stalin's atrocities. (The extended passage extolling the hero whom the reader knows to be Stalin is one of the more intricate bits of irony in the book).<br /><br />The transformations we witness continue to spiral into a void of tangled emotions: Power is the key, and it doesn't require magic. It merely requires the will to act. Emotions like the desire for vengeance can fuel such action, he learns from Mitka. At an orphanage center, he and another boy discover a railway switch. If shifted, any passing train will be diverted to a path of destruction. <i> ‚ÄúTo be capable of deciding the fate of many people whom one did not even know was a magnificent sensation. I was not sure whether the pleasure depended only on the knowledge of the power one had, or on its use.‚Äù</i> (Location 3045)<br /><br />The writing is powerful. Here is Kosinski describing the boy's passage down an icy river. <i> ‚ÄúThe current carried me toward a shore obscured by a deepening shadow. I began to discern the marshes, the tall swaying clumps of rushes, the hidden nests of sleeping ducks. The bladder moved slowly through the scattered tufts of grass. Waterflies hovered nervously on every side of me. The yellow chalices of lilies rustled, and a frightened frog belched from a ditch. Suddenly a reed pierced the bladder. I stood on the spongy bottom‚Äù.</i> (Location 585)<br /> <br />I was deep into this book before I realized there would be no traditional light at the end of the tunnel. The ending Kosinski does provide is actually dismissive of the reader's hopes. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Yes, the boy is reunited with his parents. Yes, he regains his power of speech, which he lost during one of the horrific episodes of abuse. But, there is no indication of hopefulness in the language of this ending. It is as if Kosinski is throwing the reader a bone to contrast this ‚Äúfictional‚Äù formality to the real world that fills the rest of his book. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />This was a relentlessly ugly book, just as Kosinski intended. In an afterword to the second edition, he describes his reaction to living in Switzerland in 1963. He was struck by the comfortable complacency of the emigree community. With their assets secured by Swiss banks, and their lives protected by the wartime posture of Swiss neutrality, they were indifferent to the atrocities committed across Eastern Europe. Many were even Holocaust deniers. He wrote THE PAINTED BIRD to confront that passivity. <br /><br />By conceiving a novel of relentless horror, he denies the reader the space for distancing. In his landscape there can be no particularization: a single historic anomaly, specific villains to be blamed, localized prejudices unleashed. Through the considerable power of his fiction, he is making a bleak statement about human nature. He challenges us: Prove me wrong he seems to declare. It's a difficult proposition in the light of subsequent history. Yet, it is also a challenge ‚Äî to affirm our belief in humanity through engagement.<br /><br />NOTES:<br />I read this book after reading Kosinski's much more accessible book, BEING THERE<br /><br />This blog entry makes a comparison between Kosinski and Gorky that I found interesting<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://spoilerdessert.blogspot.com/2012/01/review-of-painted-bird-jerzy-kosinski.html">http://spoilerdessert.blogspot.com/20...</a><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4408357784082354706" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('n14UW6y5bJqnRQVzHz6/aUtelsDuFfzOmvYUgEZNqtoGiTaMCC7jYJd8k7ktXVgCWoT/gSlNFvwb/fuuiF76bA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('5QyRR4xCx0C3n0Uw5N/p1xvZlGe9nNCjvvn3TDEJJux827OQKNVIuoem0/rWvA68CgP9JnrEOpE/8hhi/xp2Wg==')})
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/11121.Jerzy_Kosi_ski">About Jerzy Kosi≈Ñski</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/11121.Jerzy_Kosi_ski"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1342908489p3/11121.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/11121.Jerzy_Kosi_ski">Jerzy Kosi≈Ñski

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer17939934338342487007">Kosi≈Ñski was born Josef Lewinkopf to Jewish parents in ≈Å√≥d≈∫, Poland. As a child during World War II, he lived in central Poland under a false identity his father gave him to use, Jerzy Kosi≈Ñski. A Roman Catholic priest issued him a forged baptismal certificate. The Kosi≈Ñski family survived the Holocaust thanks to local villagers, who offered assistance to Jewish Poles often at great personal risk </span>
  <span id="freeText17939934338342487007" style="display:none">Kosi≈Ñski was born Josef Lewinkopf to Jewish parents in ≈Å√≥d≈∫, Poland. As a child during World War II, he lived in central Poland under a false identity his father gave him to use, Jerzy Kosi≈Ñski. A Roman Catholic priest issued him a forged baptismal certificate. The Kosi≈Ñski family survived the Holocaust thanks to local villagers, who offered assistance to Jewish Poles often at great personal risk (the penalty for assisting Jews in Nazi-occupied Poland was death). Kosi≈Ñski's father received help not only from Polish town leaders and churchmen, but also from individuals such as Marianna Pasiowa, a member of the Polish underground network helping Jews to evade capture. The family lived openly in DƒÖbrowa Rzeczycka near Stalowa Wola, and attended church in nearby Wola Rzeczycka, obtaining support from villagers in Kƒôpa Rzeczycka. They were sheltered temporarily by a Catholic family in Rzeczyca OkrƒÖg≈Ça. The young Jerzy even served as an altar boy in a local church.<br /><br />After World War II, Kosi≈Ñski remained with his parents in Poland, moved to Jelenia G√≥ra, and earned degrees in history and political science at the University of ≈Å√≥d≈∫. He worked as an assistant in Institute of History and Sociology at the Polish Academy of Sciences. In 1957, he emigrated to the United States, creating a fake foundation which supposedly sponsored him; he later claimed that the letters from eminent Polish communist authorities guaranteeing his loyal return, which were needed for anyone leaving the communist country at that time, had all been forged by him.<br /><br />After taking odd jobs to get by, such as driving a truck, Kosi≈Ñski graduated from Columbia University. In 1965, he became an American citizen. He received grants from Guggenheim Fellowship in 1967, Ford Foundation in 1968, and the American Academy in 1970, which allowed him to write a political non-fiction book, opening new doors of opportunity. In the States he became a lecturer at Yale, Princeton, Davenport University, and Wesleyan.<br /><br />In 1962 Kosi≈Ñski married Mary Hayward Weir who was 10 years his senior. They were divorced in 1966. Weir died in 1968 from brain cancer. Kosi≈Ñski was left nothing in her will. He later fictionalized this marriage in his novel Blind Date speaking of Weir under pseudonym Mary-Jane Kirkland. Kosi≈Ñski went on to marry Katherina "Kiki" von Fraunhofer, a marketing consultant and descendant of Bavarian aristocracy. They met in 1968.<br /><br /><b>Death</b><br /><br />Kosi≈Ñski suffered from multiple illnesses towards the end of his life, and was under attack from journalists who alleged he was a plagiarist. By the time he reached his late 50s, Kosi≈Ñski was suffering from an irregular heartbeat as well as severe physical and nervous exhaustion. Kosi≈Ñski committed suicide on May 3, 1991, by taking a fatal dose of barbiturates. His parting suicide note read: "I am going to put myself to sleep now for a bit longer than usual. Call it Eternity".</span>
  <a data-text-id="17939934338342487007" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
